<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Hammurabi</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Sixth king of Babylon (r. 1792–1750 BC)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Hammurabi_(disambiguation)" title="Hammurabi (disambiguation)">Hammurabi (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Hamurabi" redirects here. For the video game, see <a href="/wiki/Hamurabi_(video_game)" title="Hamurabi (video game)"><i>Hamurabi</i> (video game)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox vcard"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above fn" colspan="2" style="background-color: #cbe; font-size: 125%">Hammurabi<br/><span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text">𒄩𒄠𒈬𒊏𒁉</span></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-subheader" colspan="2"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Babylon" title="King of Babylon">King of Babylon</a><br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_the_Four_Corners_of_the_World" title="King of the Four Corners of the World">King of the Four Corners of the World</a></i></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image photo" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg"><img alt="F0182 Louvre Code Hammourabi Bas-relief Sb8 rwk.jpg" data-file-height="3500" data-file-width="3170" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg/220px-F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg/330px-F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg/440px-F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a><div class="infobox-caption" style="line-height:normal;padding-bottom:0.2em;padding-top:0.2em;">Hammurabi (standing), depicted as receiving his royal insignia from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">Shamash</a> (or possibly <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a>). Hammurabi holds his hands over his mouth as a sign of prayer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> (relief on the upper part of the stele of <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">Hammurabi's code of laws</a>).</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #e4dcf6;line-height:normal;padding:0.2em 0.2em"><a href="/wiki/List_of_kings_of_Babylon" title="List of kings of Babylon">King</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Babylonian_Empire" title="Old Babylonian Empire">Old Babylonian Empire</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Reign</th><td class="infobox-data">42 years; c. 1792 – c. 1750 BC</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Predecessor</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Sin-Muballit" title="Sin-Muballit">Sin-Muballit</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Successor</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Samsu-iluna" title="Samsu-iluna">Samsu-iluna</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #e4dcf6;line-height:normal;padding:0.2em 0.2em"><div style="height: 4px; width:100%;"></div></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Born</th><td class="infobox-data">c. 1810 BC<br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Babylonian_Empire" title="First Babylonian Empire">Babylon</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Died</th><td class="infobox-data">c. 1750 BC<br/>Babylon</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Issue</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Samsu-iluna" title="Samsu-iluna">Samsu-iluna</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Hammurabi</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1810</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1750 BC</span>) was the sixth Amorite king of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Babylonian_Empire" title="Old Babylonian Empire">Old Babylonian Empire</a>, reigning from c. 1792 BC to c. 1750 BC. He was preceded by his father, <a href="/wiki/Sin-Muballit" title="Sin-Muballit">Sin-Muballit</a>, who abdicated due to failing health. During his reign, he conquered <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> and the city-states of <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">Eshnunna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mari,_Syria" title="Mari, Syria">Mari</a>. He ousted <a href="/wiki/Ishme-Dagan_I" title="Ishme-Dagan I">Ishme-Dagan I</a>, the king of <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, and forced his son <a href="/wiki/Mut-Ashkur" title="Mut-Ashkur">Mut-Ashkur</a> to pay tribute, bringing almost all of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> under <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a> rule.
</p><p>Hammurabi is best known for having issued the <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">Code of Hammurabi</a>, which he claimed to have received from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">Shamash</a>, the Babylonian god of justice. Unlike earlier <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> law codes, such as the <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Ur-Nammu" title="Code of Ur-Nammu">Code of Ur-Nammu</a>, which had focused on compensating the victim of the crime, the Law of Hammurabi was one of the first law codes to place greater emphasis on the physical punishment of the perpetrator. It prescribed specific penalties for each crime and is among the first codes to establish the <a href="/wiki/Presumption_of_innocence" title="Presumption of innocence">presumption of innocence</a>. Although its penalties are extremely harsh by modern standards, they were intended to limit what a wronged person was permitted to do in <a href="/wiki/Retributive_justice" title="Retributive justice">retribution</a>. The Code of Hammurabi and the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Moses" title="Law of Moses">Law of Moses</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> contain numerous similarities.
</p><p>Hammurabi was seen by many as a god within his own lifetime. After his death, Hammurabi was revered as a great conqueror who spread civilization and forced all peoples to pay obeisance to <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_god" title="National god">national god</a> of the Babylonians. Later, his military accomplishments became de-emphasized and his role as the ideal lawgiver became the primary aspect of his legacy. For later Mesopotamians, Hammurabi's reign became the frame of reference for all events occurring in the distant past. Even after the empire he built collapsed, he was still revered as a model ruler, and many kings across the Near East claimed him as an ancestor. Hammurabi was rediscovered by archaeologists in the late nineteenth century and has since been seen as an important figure in the history of law.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_and_conquests">Reign and conquests</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hammurabi%27s_Babylonia_1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="655" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Hammurabi%27s_Babylonia_1.svg/220px-Hammurabi%27s_Babylonia_1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Hammurabi%27s_Babylonia_1.svg/330px-Hammurabi%27s_Babylonia_1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Hammurabi%27s_Babylonia_1.svg/440px-Hammurabi%27s_Babylonia_1.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hammurabi%27s_Babylonia_1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the Babylonian territory upon Hammurabi's ascension in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1792 BC</span> and upon his death in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1750 BC</span></div></div></div>
<p>Hammurabi was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorite" title="Amorite">Amorite</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Babylonian_dynasty" title="First Babylonian dynasty">First Dynasty</a> king of the <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-state</a> of Babylon, and inherited the power from his father, <a href="/wiki/Sin-Muballit" title="Sin-Muballit">Sin-Muballit</a>, in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1792 BC</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> Babylon was one of the many largely Amorite ruled city-states that dotted the central and southern Mesopotamian plains and waged war on each other for control of fertile <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural</a> land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> Though many cultures co-existed in Mesopotamia, Babylonian culture gained a degree of prominence among the <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literate</a> classes throughout the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> under Hammurabi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VM3_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-VM3-5">[4]</a></sup> The kings who came before Hammurabi had founded a relatively minor City State in 1894 BC, which controlled little territory outside of the city itself. Babylon was overshadowed by older, larger, and more powerful kingdoms such as <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">Eshnunna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a> for a century or so after its founding. However, his father <a href="/wiki/Sin-Muballit" title="Sin-Muballit">Sin-Muballit</a> had begun to consolidate rule of a small area of south central Mesopotamia under Babylonian <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> and, by the time of his reign, had conquered the minor city-states of <a href="/wiki/Borsippa" title="Borsippa">Borsippa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">Kish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sippar" title="Sippar">Sippar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VM3_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-VM3-5">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thus Hammurabi ascended to the throne as the king of a minor kingdom in the midst of a complex <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">geopolitical</a> situation. The powerful kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">Eshnunna</a> controlled the upper Tigris River while <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a> controlled the river delta. To the east of Mesopotamia lay the powerful kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a>, which regularly invaded and forced tribute upon the small states of southern Mesopotamia. In northern Mesopotamia, the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_I" title="Shamshi-Adad I">Shamshi-Adad I</a>, who had already inherited centuries old Assyrian colonies in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, had expanded his territory into the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and central <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> although his untimely death would somewhat fragment his empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first few years of Hammurabi's reign were quite peaceful. Hammurabi used his power to undertake a series of public works, including heightening the city walls for defensive purposes, and expanding the temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> In <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1701 BC</span>, the powerful kingdom of Elam, which straddled important trade routes across the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a>, invaded the Mesopotamian plain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> With allies among the plain states, Elam attacked and destroyed the kingdom of Eshnunna, destroying a number of cities and imposing its rule on portions of the plain for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Votive_monument_to_Hammurabi_BM_22454_n01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2607" data-file-width="2606" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Votive_monument_to_Hammurabi_BM_22454_n01.jpg/220px-Votive_monument_to_Hammurabi_BM_22454_n01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Votive_monument_to_Hammurabi_BM_22454_n01.jpg/330px-Votive_monument_to_Hammurabi_BM_22454_n01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Votive_monument_to_Hammurabi_BM_22454_n01.jpg/440px-Votive_monument_to_Hammurabi_BM_22454_n01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Votive_monument_to_Hammurabi_BM_22454_n01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> votive monument from Sippar, Iraq, dating to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1792</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1750 BC</span> showing King Hammurabi raising his right arm in worship, now held in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>In order to consolidate its position, Elam tried to start a war between Hammurabi's Babylonian kingdom and the kingdom of Larsa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VM18_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-VM18-11">[10]</a></sup> Hammurabi and the king of Larsa made an alliance when they discovered this duplicity and were able to crush the Elamites, although Larsa did not contribute greatly to the military effort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VM18_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-VM18-11">[10]</a></sup> Angered by Larsa's failure to come to his aid, Hammurabi turned on that southern power, thus gaining control of the entirety of the lower Mesopotamian plain by <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1763 BC</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VM31_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-VM31-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>As Hammurabi was assisted during the war in the south by his allies from the north such as <a href="/wiki/Yamhad" title="Yamhad">Yamhad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mari,_Syria" title="Mari, Syria">Mari</a>, the absence of soldiers in the north led to unrest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VM31_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-VM31-12">[11]</a></sup> Continuing his expansion, Hammurabi turned his attention northward, quelling the unrest and soon after crushing Eshnunna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Next the Babylonian armies conquered the remaining northern states, including Babylon's former ally Mari, although it is possible that the conquest of Mari was a surrender without any actual conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ar45_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ar45-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hammurabi entered into a protracted war with <a href="/wiki/Ishme-Dagan_I" title="Ishme-Dagan I">Ishme-Dagan I</a> of Assyria for control of Mesopotamia, with both kings making alliances with minor states in order to gain the upper hand. Eventually Hammurabi prevailed, ousting Ishme-Dagan I just before his own death. <a href="/wiki/Mut-Ashkur" title="Mut-Ashkur">Mut-Ashkur</a>, the new king of Assyria, was forced to pay tribute to Hammurabi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In just a few years, Hammurabi succeeded in uniting all of Mesopotamia under his rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ar45_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ar45-16">[15]</a></sup> The Assyrian kingdom survived but was forced to pay tribute during his reign, and of the major city-states in the region, only <a href="/wiki/Yamhad" title="Yamhad">Aleppo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qatna" title="Qatna">Qatna</a> to the west in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> maintained their independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ar45_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ar45-16">[15]</a></sup> However, one stele of Hammurabi has been found as far north as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diyarbekir" title="Diyarbekir">Diyarbekir</a>, where he claims the title "King of the Amorites".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vast numbers of contract <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">tablets</a>, dated to the reigns of Hammurabi and his successors, have been discovered, as well as 55 of his own letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> These letters give a glimpse into the daily trials of ruling an empire, from dealing with floods and mandating changes to a flawed <a href="/wiki/Calendar" title="Calendar">calendar</a>, to taking care of Babylon's massive herds of livestock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> Hammurabi died and passed the reins of the empire on to his son <a href="/wiki/Samsu-iluna" title="Samsu-iluna">Samsu-iluna</a> in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1750 BC</span>, under whose rule the Babylonian empire quickly began to unravel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Code_of_laws">Code of laws</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">Code of Hammurabi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P1050763_Louvre_code_Hammurabi_face_rwk.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1215" decoding="async" height="362" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/P1050763_Louvre_code_Hammurabi_face_rwk.JPG/220px-P1050763_Louvre_code_Hammurabi_face_rwk.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/P1050763_Louvre_code_Hammurabi_face_rwk.JPG/330px-P1050763_Louvre_code_Hammurabi_face_rwk.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/P1050763_Louvre_code_Hammurabi_face_rwk.JPG/440px-P1050763_Louvre_code_Hammurabi_face_rwk.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P1050763_Louvre_code_Hammurabi_face_rwk.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">Code of Hammurabi</a> stele. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Louvre" title="The Louvre">Louvre Museum</a>, Paris</div></div></div>
<p>The Code of Hammurabi is not the earliest surviving law code;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies2003_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2003-22">[21]</a></sup> it is predated by the <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Ur-Nammu" title="Code of Ur-Nammu">Code of Ur-Nammu</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_Eshnunna" title="Laws of Eshnunna">Laws of Eshnunna</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lipit-Ishtar" title="Lipit-Ishtar">Code of Lipit-Ishtar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies2003_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2003-22">[21]</a></sup> Nonetheless, the Code of Hammurabi shows marked differences from these earlier law codes and ultimately proved more influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breasted2003_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Breasted2003-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertman2003_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bertman2003-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies2003_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2003-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Code of Hammurabi was inscribed on a <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> and placed in a public place so that all could see it, although it is thought that few were literate. The stele was later plundered by the Elamites and removed to their capital, <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>; it was rediscovered there in 1901 in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and is now in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>. The code of Hammurabi contains 282 laws, written by <a href="/wiki/Scribe" title="Scribe">scribes</a> on 12 tablets. Unlike earlier laws, it was written in <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a>, the daily language of Babylon, and could therefore be read by any literate person in the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breasted2003_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Breasted2003-23">[22]</a></sup> Earlier Sumerian law codes had focused on compensating the victim of the crime,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertman2003_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bertman2003-24">[23]</a></sup> but the Code of Hammurabi instead focused on physically punishing the perpetrator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertman2003_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bertman2003-24">[23]</a></sup> The Code of Hammurabi was one of the first law codes to place restrictions on what a wronged person was allowed to do in <a href="/wiki/Retributive_justice" title="Retributive justice">retribution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertman2003_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bertman2003-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The structure of the code is very specific, with each offense receiving a specified punishment. The punishments tended to be very harsh by modern standards, with many offenses resulting in death, disfigurement, or the use of the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eye_for_an_Eye" title="Eye for an Eye">Eye for eye, tooth for tooth</a>" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/An_eye_for_an_eye" title="An eye for an eye">Lex Talionis</a> "Law of Retaliation") philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertman2003_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bertman2003-24">[23]</a></sup> The code is also one of the earliest examples of the idea of <a href="/wiki/Presumption_of_innocence" title="Presumption of innocence">presumption of innocence</a>, and it also suggests that the accused and accuser have the opportunity to provide <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> However, there is no provision for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extenuating_circumstances" title="Extenuating circumstances">extenuating circumstances</a> to alter the prescribed punishment.
</p><p>A carving at the top of the stele portrays Hammurabi receiving the laws from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">Shamash</a>, the Babylonian god of justice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> and the preface states that Hammurabi was chosen by Shamash to bring the laws to the people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> Parallels between this narrative and the giving of the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_Code" title="Covenant Code">Covenant Code</a> to <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> by <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">Yahweh</a> atop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_Mount_Sinai" title="Biblical Mount Sinai">Mount Sinai</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Biblical</a> <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Book of Exodus</a> and similarities between the two legal codes suggest a common ancestor in the Semitic background of the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Douglas_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Douglas-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton_406_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barton_406-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unger_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unger-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Free_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Free-32">[31]</a></sup> Nonetheless, fragments of previous law codes have been found and it is unlikely that the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Moses" title="Law of Moses">Mosaic laws</a> were directly inspired by the Code of Hammurabi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Douglas_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Douglas-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton_406_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barton_406-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unger_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Unger-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Free_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Free-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[b]</a></sup> Some scholars have disputed this; David P. Wright argues that the Jewish Covenant Code is "directly, primarily, and throughout" based upon the Laws of Hammurabi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> In 2010, a team of archaeologists from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew_University" title="Hebrew University">Hebrew University</a> discovered a cuneiform tablet dating to the eighteenth or seventeenth century BC at <a href="/wiki/Tel_Hazor" title="Tel Hazor">Hazor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> containing laws clearly derived from the Code of Hammurabi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Posthumous_commemoration">Posthumous commemoration</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Babylonian_tablet_of_Hammurabi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2082" data-file-width="1165" decoding="async" height="429" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Babylonian_tablet_of_Hammurabi.jpg/240px-Babylonian_tablet_of_Hammurabi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Babylonian_tablet_of_Hammurabi.jpg/360px-Babylonian_tablet_of_Hammurabi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Babylonian_tablet_of_Hammurabi.jpg/480px-Babylonian_tablet_of_Hammurabi.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Babylonian_tablet_of_Hammurabi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tablet of Hammurabi (<span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text">𒄩𒄠𒈬𒊏𒁉</span>, 4th line from the top), King of Babylon. British Museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Hammurabi was honored above all other kings of the second millennium BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005128_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005128-39">[37]</a></sup> and he received the unique honor of being declared to be a god within his own lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005127_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005127-40">[38]</a></sup> The personal name "Hammurabi-ili" meaning "Hammurabi is my god" became common during and after his reign. In writings from shortly after his death, Hammurabi is commemorated mainly for three achievements: bringing victory in war, bringing peace, and bringing justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005127_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005127-40">[38]</a></sup> Hammurabi's conquests came to be regarded as part of a sacred mission to spread civilization to all nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005126_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005126-41">[39]</a></sup> A stele from Ur glorifies him in his own voice as a mighty ruler who forces evil into submission and compels all peoples to worship <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005126–127_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005126–127-42">[40]</a></sup> The stele declares: "The people of Elam, Gutium, Subartu, and Tukrish, whose mountains are distant and whose languages are obscure, I placed into [Marduk's] hand. I myself continued to put straight their confused minds." A later hymn also written in Hammurabi's own voice extols him as a powerful, supernatural force for Marduk:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005126_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005126-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><div class="poem">
<p>I am the king, the brace that grasps wrongdoers, that makes people of one mind,<br/>
I am the great dragon among kings, who throws their counsel in disarray,<br/>
I am the net that is stretched over the enemy,<br/>
I am the fear-inspiring, who, when lifting his fierce eyes, gives the disobedient the death sentence,<br/>
I am the great net that covers evil intent,<br/>
I am the young lion, who breaks nets and scepters,<br/>
I am the battle net that catches him who offends me.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005126–127_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005126–127-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<p>After extolling Hammurabi's military accomplishments, the hymn finally declares: "I am Hammurabi, the king of justice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005127_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005127-40">[38]</a></sup> In later commemorations, Hammurabi's role as a great lawgiver came to be emphasized above all his other accomplishments and his military achievements became de-emphasized. Hammurabi's reign became the point of reference for all events in the distant past. A hymn to the goddess <a href="/wiki/Inanna" title="Inanna">Ishtar</a>, whose language suggests it was written during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ammi-Saduqa" title="Ammi-Saduqa">Ammisaduqa</a>, Hammurabi's fourth successor, declares: "The king who first heard this song as a song of your heroism is Hammurabi. This song for you was composed in his reign. May he be given life forever!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005128_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005128-39">[37]</a></sup> For centuries after his death, Hammurabi's laws continued to be copied by scribes as part of their writing exercises and they were even partially translated into Sumerian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005129_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005129-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_legacy">Political legacy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Babylonian_stele_Louvre_Sb9.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1354" data-file-width="894" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Babylonian_stele_Louvre_Sb9.jpg/170px-Babylonian_stele_Louvre_Sb9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Babylonian_stele_Louvre_Sb9.jpg/255px-Babylonian_stele_Louvre_Sb9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Babylonian_stele_Louvre_Sb9.jpg/340px-Babylonian_stele_Louvre_Sb9.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Babylonian_stele_Louvre_Sb9.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copy of Hammurabi's stele usurped by <a href="/wiki/Shutruk-Nakhunte" title="Shutruk-Nakhunte">Shutruk-Nahhunte I</a>. The stele was only partially erased and was never re-inscribed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005129–130_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005129–130-44">[42]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>During the reign of Hammurabi, Babylon usurped the position of "most holy city" in southern Mesopotamia from its predecessor, <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneider_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider-45">[43]</a></sup> Under the rule of Hammurabi's successor <a href="/wiki/Samsu-iluna" title="Samsu-iluna">Samsu-iluna</a>, the short-lived Babylonian Empire began to collapse. In northern Mesopotamia, both the Amorites and Babylonians were driven from <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> by <a href="/wiki/Puzur-Sin" title="Puzur-Sin">Puzur-Sin</a> a native <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a>-speaking ruler, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1740 BC</span>. Around the same time, native Akkadian speakers threw off Amorite Babylonian rule in the far south of Mesopotamia, creating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sealand_Dynasty" title="Sealand Dynasty">Sealand Dynasty</a>, in more or less the region of ancient Sumer. Hammurabi's ineffectual successors met with further defeats and loss of territory at the hands of Assyrian kings such as <a href="/wiki/Adasi" title="Adasi">Adasi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bel-ibni" title="Bel-ibni">Bel-ibni</a>, as well as to the Sealand Dynasty to the south, <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> to the east, and to the <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassites</a> from the northeast. Thus was Babylon quickly reduced to the small and minor state it had once been upon its founding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>coup de grace</i> for the Hammurabi's Amorite Dynasty occurred in 1595 BC, when Babylon was sacked and conquered by the powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" title="Hittite Empire">Hittite Empire</a>, thereby ending all Amorite political presence in Mesopotamia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D19_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-D19-47">[45]</a></sup> However, the Indo-European-speaking Hittites did not remain, turning over Babylon to their <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassite</a> allies, a people speaking a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zagros_mountains" title="Zagros mountains">Zagros mountains</a> region. This <a href="/wiki/Kardunia%C5%A1" title="Karduniaš">Kassite Dynasty</a> ruled Babylon for over 400 years and adopted many aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a>, including Hammurabi's code of laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D19_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-D19-47">[45]</a></sup> Even after the fall of the Amorite Dynasty, however, Hammurabi was still remembered and revered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005129_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005129-43">[41]</a></sup> When the Elamite king <a href="/wiki/Shutruk-Nakhunte" title="Shutruk-Nakhunte">Shutruk-Nahhunte I</a> raided Babylon in 1158 BC and carried off many stone monuments, he had most of the inscriptions on these monuments erased and new inscriptions carved into them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005129_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005129-43">[41]</a></sup> On the stele containing Hammurabi's laws, however, only four or five columns were wiped out and no new inscription was ever added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005129–130_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005129–130-44">[42]</a></sup> Over a thousand years after Hammurabi's death, the kings of <a href="/wiki/Suhum" title="Suhum">Suhu</a>, a land along the Euphrates river, just northwest of Babylon, claimed him as their ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005130_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_De_Mieroop2005130-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_rediscovery">Modern rediscovery</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hammurabi_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="319" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Hammurabi_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg/170px-Hammurabi_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Hammurabi_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg/255px-Hammurabi_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6f/Hammurabi_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hammurabi_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bas-relief of Hammurabi in the US Congress</div></div></div>
<p>In the late nineteenth century, the Code of Hammurabi became a major center of debate in the heated <i>Babel und Bibel</i> ("Babylon and Bible") controversy in Germany over the relationship between the Bible and ancient Babylonian texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiolkowski201225_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiolkowski201225-49">[47]</a></sup> In January 1902, the German Assyriologist <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Delitzsch" title="Friedrich Delitzsch">Friedrich Delitzsch</a> gave a lecture at the <a href="/wiki/Sing-Akademie_zu_Berlin" title="Sing-Akademie zu Berlin">Sing-Akademie zu Berlin</a> in front of the <a href="/wiki/Kaiser" title="Kaiser">Kaiser</a> and his wife, in which he argued that the Mosaic Laws of the Old Testament were directly copied off the Code of Hammurabi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiolkowski201223–25_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiolkowski201223–25-50">[48]</a></sup> Delitzsch's lecture was so controversial that, by September 1903, he had managed to collect 1,350 short articles from newspapers and journals, over 300 longer ones, and twenty-eight pamphlets, all written in response to this lecture, as well as the preceding one about the <a href="/wiki/Gilgamesh_flood_myth" title="Gilgamesh flood myth">Flood story in the <i>Epic of Gilgamesh</i></a>. These articles were overwhelmingly critical of Delitzsch, though a few were sympathetic. The Kaiser distanced himself from Delitzsch and his radical views and, in fall of 1904, Delitzsch was forced to give his third lecture in <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankfurt_am_Main" title="Frankfurt am Main">Frankfurt am Main</a> rather than in Berlin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiolkowski201225_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiolkowski201225-49">[47]</a></sup> The putative relationship between the Mosaic Law and the Code of Hammurabi later became a major part of Delitzsch's argument in his 1920–21 book <i>Die große Täuschung</i> (<i>The Great Deception</i>) that the Hebrew Bible was irredeemably contaminated by Babylonian influence and that only by eliminating the human Old Testament entirely could Christians finally believe in the true, <a href="/wiki/Aryan" title="Aryan">Aryan</a> message of the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiolkowski201223–25_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiolkowski201223–25-50">[48]</a></sup> In the early twentieth century, many scholars believed that Hammurabi was <a href="/wiki/Amraphel" title="Amraphel">Amraphel</a>, the King of <a href="/wiki/Shinar" title="Shinar">Shinar</a> in the Book of Genesis 14:1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> This view has now been largely rejected,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Granerød2010_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Granerød2010-54">[52]</a></sup> and Amraphael's existence is not attested in any writings from outside the Bible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Granerød2010_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Granerød2010-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of Hammurabi's reputation as a lawgiver, his depiction can be found in several United States government buildings. Hammurabi is one of the 23 lawgivers depicted in <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bas-relief" title="Bas-relief">bas-reliefs</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol#House_Chamber" title="United States Capitol">chamber</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">frieze</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adolph_Weinman" title="Adolph Weinman">Adolph Weinman</a> depicting the "great lawgivers of history", including Hammurabi, is on the south wall of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court_building" title="United States Supreme Court building">U.S. Supreme Court building</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'u' in 'push'">ʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a>: <span lang="und-Xsux" style="font-size:125%;font-family:'Segoe UI Historic','Akkadian','Noto Sans Cuneiform','Noto Sans Sumero-Akkadian Cuneiform';" title="cuneiform text">𒄩𒄠𒈬𒊏𒁉</span> <i>Ḫâmmurabi</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Amorite_language" title="Amorite language">Amorite</a> <i>ʻAmmurāpi</i> ("the kinsman is a healer"), itself from <i>ʻAmmu</i> ("paternal kinsman") and <i>Rāpi</i> ("healer").</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Barton, a former professor of Semitic languages at the University of Pennsylvania, stated that while there are similarities between the two texts, a study of the entirety of both laws "convinces the student that the laws of the Old Testament are in no essential way dependent upon the Babylonian laws." He states that "such resemblances" arose from "a similarity of antecedents and of general intellectual outlook" between the two cultures, but that "the striking differences show that there was no direct borrowing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton_406_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barton_406-30">[29]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
